        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4405970" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5986240261677869266">Read 8/23-8/29/07<br/><br/>Mistress of the Art of Death tells the tale of Adelia, a "doctor to the dead" in the 12th century. Dispatched from her academic existence at the University of Salerno to medieval Cambridge to investigate the gruesome death of four <br/>children, Adelia is forced to hide her true identity and attempt to blend in with the provincial English folk. Alternately horrified and fascinated, Adelia struggles to fulfill her mission, dodging danger and deceit at every turn, and maintain her sens</span>
  <span id="freeText5986240261677869266" style="display:none">Read 8/23-8/29/07<br /><br />Mistress of the Art of Death tells the tale of Adelia, a "doctor to the dead" in the 12th century. Dispatched from her academic existence at the University of Salerno to medieval Cambridge to investigate the gruesome death of four <br />children, Adelia is forced to hide her true identity and attempt to blend in with the provincial English folk. Alternately horrified and fascinated, Adelia struggles to fulfill her mission, dodging danger and deceit at every turn, and maintain her sense of independence.<br /><br />This is one of the best books I've read all year and I highly recommend it. The writing style is infused with a dry, caustic wit that is simply marvelous. I knew on about page three that I'd love this book, for that alone. Another<br />thing I greatly admired was the character growth, for Adelia and the superb cast of secondary players in the novel. People changed and reacted and grew in subtle, surprising ways that were fascinating to watch unfold. The historical fiction elements were well-done, particularly a small yet<br />intruiging couple of appearances by Henry II. This novel is part forensic mystery, part historical fiction - the forensic pieces being rather gruesome (I skimmed that) but ultimately the different pieces mesh together to create<br />a truly fascinating story about a medieval careerwoman that resonates right down to our own time. Highly, highly recommended.<br /><br />Number : 77<br /><br />Genre : Historical Fiction<br />Publication : 2007<br />Rating : 9.25<br />Where From : the library<br />Reason : Kathleen's rec<br /><br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_33391885" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33391885" />
    <a title="Juushika" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/616321-juushika"><img alt="Juushika" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1541586729p2/616321.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33391885?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Juushika" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Juushika" href="/user/show/616321-juushika">Juushika</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=genre-historical">genre-historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/616321-juushika?shelf=status-borrowed">status-borrowed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33391885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7227643079196628583">Children are being kidnapped in Cambridge, England, and one body has been found; local Jews are being blamed for their deaths. In order to identify the true murderer, a doctor specializing in autopsies comes to Cambridge from Salernobut the doctor, Adelia, is female, and the year is 1171. A combination historical fiction and crime drama, <i>Mistress of the Art of Death</i> is a mediocre example of both: anachronisms litter the 12th Century setting, and the detective work is adequately plotted but unex</span>
  <span id="freeText7227643079196628583" style="display:none">Children are being kidnapped in Cambridge, England, and one body has been found; local Jews are being blamed for their deaths. In order to identify the true murderer, a doctor specializing in autopsies comes to Cambridge from Salernobut the doctor, Adelia, is female, and the year is 1171. A combination historical fiction and crime drama, <i>Mistress of the Art of Death</i> is a mediocre example of both: anachronisms litter the 12th Century setting, and the detective work is adequately plotted but unexceptional and forgettable. Hundreds of weaknesses dot the text, and as such I was neither pleased nor disappointed by this book: although readable, it's not good and it's far from memorable, and I don't recommend it.<br /><br />There are a number of reviews that discuss the anachronisms that fill this book. I rarely read historical fiction and know little about this time period, but I still saw these inaccuracies and found them to be distracting. Adelia is intended to be a doctor before her time, whose education and perception have made her aware of things that others are far from realizing. When Adelia amazes us once or twice, she is an intelligent, compelling character; when there is a constant stream of minor miracles (boiling water, disinfecting wounds, bathing, eating salads, diagnosing cholera and obesity, using opiates as painkillers and sleep aids) the character is instead rendered foolishly impossible and the historical setting is destroyed. This is, on the whole, how Franklin writes her book: she makes attempts at various aspects--a historical setting, character creation, romance, a murder mystery--and then foils her own plans in a variety of little weaknesses and errors. No one of these foibles is huge, and even together they don't strictly ruin the book, but they do weaken it. The suspense of the mystery is mocked when the shadowy suspect is called "it" or the killer's face is revealed and recognized--but the killer's name is withheld until the last sentence of the chapter. The unlikely romance becomes entirely unbelievable after a mess of miscommunication and assumptions. Adelia is a forward-thinking female and an impeccable doctor despite her time period--except that viewing corpses still drives her to distraction. The villain is truly horrific, but that there is no motivation for his evil except than he is "sick." Even the writing style is on the whole competent, until it is interrupted by unexplained Latin and French phrases or changes in point of view or preposition use.<br /><br />Despite all of these hundred little complaints, the book is not actually bad. The various aspects still function, the book is still readable and interesting, and on the whole it is competent if not wholly satisfying. In fact, if only one or two of the aspects were faulted, this book may even verge on good. After all, the premise has potential, and Adelia's (generally) rational, cool characterization makes her an unusual and compelling hero. Instead, the fact that every aspect lacks something, every aspect is somehow weak, makes this novel not good, not bad, but entirely mediocre. The strengths are perfectly balanced by these errors, and the result is a text so unmemorable that it is difficult to review--because I have no strong opinions one way or the other, and don't want to turn my thoughts to it. In a world with so many books that are worth reading and enjoying, I can't recommend one that I can barely think on two days after completing. By all means, if you want to, then do read this book. It goes swiftly and holds attention, and there is a certain amount of satisfaction in following the murder mystery to its conclusion (although even this is dampened by a too-long epilogue). It is wiser to borrow the book than buy it because it has limited reread potential. But I don't and can't recommend <i>Mistress of the Art of Death</i>, and ultimately I was disappointed--not because the book is poor, but because it is entirely mediocre, and left me with no redeeming factors to take away and enjoy.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7227643079196628583" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer4820236915944475245">Brilliant story telling. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_203331871" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/203331871" />
    <a title="Jackleen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5480965-jackleen"><img alt="Jackleen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1305993173p2/5480965.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/203331871?book_show_action=true">Aug 29, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jackleen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jackleen" href="/user/show/5480965-jackleen">Jackleen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5480965-jackleen?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5480965-jackleen?shelf=historical-mystery">historical-mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5480965-jackleen?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5480965-jackleen?shelf=england">england</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5480965-jackleen?shelf=middle-ages">middle-ages</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5480965-jackleen?shelf=catholic-church">catholic-church</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5480965-jackleen?shelf=medical">medical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5480965-jackleen?shelf=1200s">1200s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer203331871" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10530273070138519154">Four and a half stars.<br/>A historical thriller, The Mistress of the Art of Death, will rival modern day forensic medicine novels. The story combines medieval England in the 1200 century, crusader knights, questionable nuns, suspicious monks, and, a sly King Henry II, not to mention, four horribly mutilated dead children and the Jewish community that stands accused of these atrocities. The people of Cambridge believe the Jews have crucified their children in a passover rite. Then, add to this explos</span>
  <span id="freeText10530273070138519154" style="display:none">Four and a half stars.<br />A historical thriller, The Mistress of the Art of Death, will rival modern day forensic medicine novels. The story combines medieval England in the 1200 century, crusader knights, questionable nuns, suspicious monks, and, a sly King Henry II, not to mention, four horribly mutilated dead children and the Jewish community that stands accused of these atrocities. The people of Cambridge believe the Jews have crucified their children in a passover rite. Then, add to this explosive situation, the main character, Adelia, a woman Italian doctor, unusual in itself considering the times, who further specializes as a mistress of the art of death, the for runner to a pathologist or forensic medical examiner. An occupation not for the faint of heart. <br />Specializing in forensic anatomy, Adelia, works in this select area of medicine that is beyond most men, never mind a young woman, and excels. This chosen occupation is all well and good for Adelia, while working under the protection of the King of Sicily in the open minded cosmopolitan city of Salerno, a medical mecca of it's time. However, Adelia professional security is about to be altered in unforeseen ways. At the request of Henry II, who grieves for his lost income from his rich Jews, if not for the poor tortured children, The King of Sicily sends his best Master of the art of death, Adelia. However, Cambridge, England, is not forward in it's thinking, and Adelia must hide her talents, for fear of being taken as a witch. Joined in this espionage, is Simon of Naples, a top rate investigator and a Jew himself, sent to save the Jewish population of Cambridge, now holed up in the castle, in constant danger of attack from the town folk, outraged by the attacks on their children. The third party in this team is the Muslim eunuch, Mansur, Adelia's servant and protector, who becomes, to his surprise, the community doctor, to deflect the suspicions of the community from Adelia.<br />The three setup shop as a medical team, with Mansur as Doctor and Adelia as his assistant, while investigating the many potential murders found within the small town. The number of potential suspects is startling high, and includes prominent citizens of Cambridge, making caution the byword of Adelia, Mansur and Simon. As the three willow down the suspects, Sir Rowley Picot, a former Crusader knight, friendly and attractive, pops up unexpectedly and repeatedly at the sites of investigation, inducing both suspicion and unwanted feelings within Adelia. Rowley offers frequent aid and support within this difficult investigation and soon learns of Adelia'a secret skills. Questioning Roley's motives, Adelia, normally set apart from her colleagues in Salerno and even lonelier here in England, is confused about being attracted to a potential murder suspect.<br />Unexpected deaths and unexpected alliances add to the solid plot of a very good crime novel. Set within the historical backdrop of Cambridge, England and packed with many well sketched characters, The Mistress of the Art of Death is an Excellent who done it with class.<br />In addition, Ariana Franklin presents a vary valid reason for Henry II argument and subsequent murder of Archbishop Thomas of Canterbury that is both surprising yet so completely sensible that the reader can not help but have one of those light bulb moments so rare in literature.<br />The only detraction for this novel is the confusing number of characters that are thrust upon the reader within the first 30 pages. The initial flipping back and forth, to determine 'who is this again', cost Franklin the half star. But, reader be patient because once Franklin hits her stride in this book, you will be carried along in a surprisingly good top notch story. And, like all good detective stories, the ending is a complete surprise. So no peaking at the end. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10530273070138519154" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_7162106" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7162106" />
    <a title="LJ" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/250195-lj"><img alt="LJ" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1436467895p2/250195.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7162106?book_show_action=true">Oct 02, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="LJ" class="user" itemprop="url" name="LJ" href="/user/show/250195-lj">LJ</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780593056493" href="/book/show/605139.Mistress_of_the_Art_of_Death">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/250195-lj?shelf=historical">historical</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/250195-lj?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/250195-lj?shelf=female_author">female_author</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/250195-lj?shelf=1100s">1100s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/250195-lj?shelf=england">england</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/250195-lj?shelf=middle_ages_5th_cent-1558">middle_ages_5th_cent-1558</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7162106" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17028810390497100859">MISTRESS OF THE ART OF DEATH (Historical-Adelia Aguilar-England-1170) – Ex<br/>Franklin, Ariana (aka Diana Norman) – 1st in series<br/>Bantam Press, 2007, UK Hardcover – ISBN: 9780593056493<br/>First Sentence: Here they come.<br/>*** A child has been murdered and residents in Cambridge claim he was crucified by the Jews. The Jews provide Henry II with a large part of his revenue and requires that the real killer be quickly found. From Naples come Simon of Naples, an renowned investigator, Mansur the Saracen, and a </span>
  <span id="freeText17028810390497100859" style="display:none">MISTRESS OF THE ART OF DEATH (Historical-Adelia Aguilar-England-1170) – Ex<br />Franklin, Ariana (aka Diana Norman) – 1st in series<br />Bantam Press, 2007, UK Hardcover – ISBN: 9780593056493<br />First Sentence: Here they come.<br />*** A child has been murdered and residents in Cambridge claim he was crucified by the Jews. The Jews provide Henry II with a large part of his revenue and requires that the real killer be quickly found. From Naples come Simon of Naples, an renowned investigator, Mansur the Saracen, and a woman doctor trained in the study of corpses at the School of Medicine in Salerno, Adelia Aguilar. The bodies of other children are found, and Adelia is determined to find their killer.<br />*** I am about to gush! This book was judged the best researched of its year by the BBC and historian Dr. David Starkey. Happily, it is not written in Middle English and, as the author admits, some liberties were taken. Others more versed in this time than am I, may be able to find historic fault with it. I don’t care. I found the history fascinating and learned even more about life in this period. The style of writing was wonderful; from that first three-word sentence, I was entranced. I loved the characters; Adelia, Simon, Mansur, Ulf, Gyltha, Prior Geoffrey, Henry II (whose voice I shall always hear as Peter O’Toole’s) were real to me and others became so as the story progressed. The language was a bit challenging at first, but soon became easy to read. The sights, sounds and smells of the town were described to place me within the story. The story kept me involved from beginning to end and tapped all my emotions. There is a wonderful romance which arises to warm the heart and quicken the pulse. I laughed, cried; was moved, frightened and appalled and I can’t wait until the next book comes out next May. What can I say; I loved it.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17028810390497100859" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AIohrMv4ABAYGvkuvdk4l5M2aZzl2x5s0BDraGwIIoH3VUvMTJz4dYwzFPK54hwfoqAy6ZNQ20F1cTcFWzl3pA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('4ZaJAp4bN+DEnga0U+O8mR+tl7OMpiwUiCgbED+ZNyAWSeNiGX/PhVC362hX2JgRLjvMxvot6TktScd9CKhiBQ==')})
  }

In Money, Jacob Goldstein shows how money is a useful fiction that has shaped societies for thousands of years, from the rise of coins in ancient Greece to the first stock market in Amsterdam to the emergence of shadow banking in the 21st century.At the heart of the story are the fringe thinkers and world leaders who reimagined money. Kublai Khan, the Mongol emperor, created paper money backed by nothing, centuries before it appeared in the west. John Law, a professional gambler and convicted murderer, brought modern money to France (and destroyed the country\'s economy). The cypherpunks, a group of radical libertarian computer programmers, paved the way for bitcoin.One thing they all realized: what counts as money (and what doesn\'t) is the result of choices we make, and those choices have a profound effect on who gets more stuff and who gets less, who gets to take risks when times are good, and who gets screwed when things go bad.Lively, accessible, and full of interesting details (like the 43-pound copper coins that 17th-century Swedes carried strapped to their backs), Money is the story of the choices that gave us money as we know it today.\n  \n<\/span>\n <span id="freeTextContainer2452911803974041774">Ariana Franklin was the pen name of British writer <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16799.Diana_Norman' title='Diana Norman' rel='nofollow noopener'>Diana Norman</a>. A former journalist, Norman had written several critically acclaimed biographies and historical novels. She lived in Hertfordshire, England, with her husband, the film critic Barry Norman. <br/><br/>Note:<br/>The Death Maze (UK) is published as The Serpent's Tale in the US. <br/>Relics of the Dead (UK) is published as Grave Goods in the US.<br/>The Assassin'</span>
  <span id="freeText2452911803974041774" style="display:none">Ariana Franklin was the pen name of British writer <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/16799.Diana_Norman" title="Diana Norman" rel="nofollow noopener">Diana Norman</a>. A former journalist, Norman had written several critically acclaimed biographies and historical novels. She lived in Hertfordshire, England, with her husband, the film critic Barry Norman. <br /><br />Note:<br />The Death Maze (UK) is published as The Serpent's Tale in the US. <br />Relics of the Dead (UK) is published as Grave Goods in the US.<br />The Assassin's Prayer (UK) is published as A Murderous Procession in the US. <br /><br /><br /></span>

The arsonist remains at large, but the fire has uncovered something even more shocking: two hidden skeletons, a man and a woman. As word of her healing powers has spread, so have rumors of witchcraft. But when Allie arrives, all is chaos. A village girl has disappeared—and she’s not the first. Over the past few months, several girls from the villages surrounding Ely have vanished. When the body of one of the missing is discovered, Allie manages to examine the remains before burial. The results lead her to suspect that a monstrous predator is on the loose. Will her training and her stubborn pursuit of the truth help her find the killer . . . or make her the next victim?\n\nA richly detailed, twisty thriller, Death and the Maiden is historical mystery at
